   "source": [
    "In this project we are analyzing the NOAA data set which we originally found here: https://catalog.data.gov/dataset/u-s-daily-climate-normals-1981-2010\n",
    "\n",
    "After browsing their website, we came across their suite of data on Google BigQuery of environmental and climatic data from 1929-2017. This data set includes \"meteorological parameters for thousands of U.S. stations located across the 50 states, as well as U.S. territories, commonwealths, the Compact of Free Association nations, and one station in Canada.\" Such data can include: temperature, air pressure, preciptation, severe weather events, Wind Speed, Dew Point, etc...\n",
    "\n",
    "For this project we include every tenth year starting at 1929 and going until 2017 (note: we go from 2009 to 2017 even though that is not a ten year jump - just so we can see the most recent data as well).\n",
    "\n",
    "As another note, we include sample sizes of 2000 from each of the years because otherwise our data would be too big for Jupyter to handle. So the code below essentially queries Google BigQuery's API and processes them all into one large data frame of 20,000 rows containing all of the data. We will do some post processing afterwards. \n",
    "\n",
    "***Note: There are more columns in this dataFrame than Pandas can show. A full list of the columns and their data is included here in the bottom table (under \"Table Details\"): https://bigquery.cloud.google.com/table/bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1929?tab=schema\n",
    "\n",
    "For the first step, we import data from Google, and put it into dataframes."
   ]

   "source": [
    "import pandas as pd\n",
    "from pandas.io import gbq\n",
    "import sklearn\n",
    "from scipy.stats import mstats\n",
    "import numpy.ma as ma\n",
    "import matplotlib.pyplot as plt\n",
    "from scipy import stats\n",
    "import numpy as np\n",
    "from ggplot import *\n",
    "import matplotlib.pyplot as pyplot\n",
    "import matplotlib.patches as mpatches\n",
    "from sklearn.decomposition import PCA\n",
    "np.warnings.filterwarnings('ignore')\n",
    "\n",
    "dataFrame = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1929] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "\n",
    "dF2 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1939] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF2)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF3 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1949] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF3)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF4 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1959] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF4)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF5 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1969] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF5)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF55 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1979] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF55)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF6 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1989] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF6)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF7 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod1999] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF7)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF8 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod2009] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF8)\n",
    "\n",
    "dF9 = gbq.read_gbq(\"SELECT * FROM [bigquery-public-data:noaa_gsod.gsod2017] LIMIT 2000\", project_id=\"finalproject-167316\")\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.append(dF9)\n",
    "\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.rename(columns={'stn': 'Station Number'\n",
    "                        , 'year': 'Year'\n",
    "                        , 'mo': 'Month'\n",
    "                        , 'da': 'Day'\n",
    "                        , 'temp': 'Temperature'\n",
    "                        , 'dewp': 'Dew Point'\n",
    "                        , 'slp': 'Sea Level Pressure'\n",
    "                        , 'stp': 'Station Pressure'\n",
    "                        , 'wdsp': 'Wind Speed'\n",
    "                        , 'mxpsd': 'Max Wind Speed'\n",
    "                        , 'max': 'Maximum Temperature'\n",
    "                        , 'min': 'Minimum Temperature'\n",
    "                        , 'prcp': 'Daily Preciptation'\n",
    "                        , 'sndp': 'Snow Accumulation'})\n",
    "\n",
    "dataFrame = dataFrame.drop('wban', 1)"
   ]

   "source": [
    "We want to ensure that viewers of this data do not confuse the \"99.99\" for Daily Precipitation and \"999.9\" for Snow Accumulation as actual data. These essentially mean there was no preciptation or accumulation on that given day so we do some post-processing here and set them to 0.0 each respectively. We also set some of the columns to contain nunmerical values instead of string values for an eaiser analysis."
   ]

    "timeArray = ['1929', '1939', '1949', '1959', '1969', '1979', '1989', '1999', '2009', '2017']\n",
    "timeArrayInt = [1929, 1939, 1949, 1959, 1969, 1979, 1989, 1999, 2009, 2017]\n",
    "\n",
    "one = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1929', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "two = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1939', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "three = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1949', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "four = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1959', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "five = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1969', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "six = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1979', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "seven = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1989', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "eight = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1999', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "nine = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2009', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "ten = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2017', 'Temperature'])\n",
    "\n",
    "overallMeanArray = [one, two, three, four,\n",
    "                   five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten]\n",
    "\n",
    "x = timeArrayInt\n",
    "xi = np.asarray(timeArrayInt)\n",
    "slope, intercept, r_value, p_value, std_err = mstats.linregress(x,overallMeanArray)\n",
    "line1 = intercept + slope*xi\n",
    "\n",
    "overallMeanTempArray = overallMeanArray;\n",
    "\n",
    "plt.figure(figsize=(11,5))\n",
    "plt.plot(timeArray, overallMeanArray, '-b', linewidth=1.5, label='') #blue line\n",
    "plt.plot(xi,line1,'g-')\n",
    "plt.ylabel('Temperature in Fahrenheit')\n",
    "plt.xlabel('Year')\n",
    "plt.title('Mean Temperature over Time')\n",
    "plt.show()"
   ]

   "source": [
    "As this graph shows, the temperature took a dip around 1960 and 1980. However, the linear regression of this line shows that the temperature is in fact slowly increasing over time. While these large dips are shown here, it may just be due to our limited sample size. These weather locations are scattered around the country randomly in our data set and it could be that we happened to get a bunch of colder location stations for those time periods. However, this sample should provide some insight that the temperature has in fact been increasing since the late 1920s."

    "one = ma.mean(oneArr)\n",

    "                   five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten]\n",
    "\n",
    "x = timeArrayInt\n",
    "xi = np.asarray(timeArrayInt)\n",
    "slope, intercept, r_value, p_value, std_err = mstats.linregress(x,overallMeanArray)\n",
    "line1 = intercept + slope*xi\n",
    "\n",
    "overallMeanWindArray = overallMeanArray\n",
    "\n",
    "plt.figure(figsize=(11,5))\n",
    "plt.plot(timeArray, overallMeanArray, '-b', linewidth=1.5, label='') #blue line\n",

    "one = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1929', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "two = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1939', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "three = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1949', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "four = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1959', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "five = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1969', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "six = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1979', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "seven = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1989', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "eight = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1999', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "nine = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2009', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "ten = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2017', 'Daily Preciptation'])\n",
    "\n",
    "overallMeanArray = [one, two, three, four,\n",
    "                   five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten]\n",
    "\n",
    "x = timeArrayInt\n",
    "xi = np.asarray(timeArrayInt)\n",
    "slope, intercept, r_value, p_value, std_err = mstats.linregress(x,overallMeanArray)\n",
    "line1 = intercept + slope*xi\n",
    "\n",
    "overallPrecipMeanArray = overallMeanArray\n",
    "\n",
    "plt.figure(figsize=(11,5))\n",
    "plt.plot(timeArray, overallMeanArray, '-b', linewidth=1.5, label='') #blue line\n",
    "plt.plot(xi,line1,'g-')\n",
    "plt.ylabel('Preciptation in Inches')\n",
    "plt.xlabel('Year')\n",
    "plt.title('Precipitation over Time')\n",
    "plt.show()"

   "source": [
    "This graph is certainly interesting. It is plotting the mean preciptation over time, however it jumps signifigantly between 1960 and 1980. This could mean one of two things: 1. Either the sample we choose for 1959-1979 were in more precipitous regions or 2. There was some widespread weather event for the course of 20 years that caused more rainfall/snowfall to happen between that time period. Regardless, the precipitation during the other time periods was relatively consistent, however has been picking up slightly since 2009. In order to get a better idea, let's look at the distribution of rainfall for each year using a violin plot."

    "This code and graph's goal is to break down severe weather events such as hail, thunder, and tornados since 1929. The idea is to see whether or not there was a time period in which severe weather increased or decreased. As our graph shows, the total number of severe weather events has increased over time. 2009 clearly had the most severe weather events and the reason 2017's is small is because it only includes data up until early May. \n",
    "\n",

    "The goal of this code and graph is to show during which months severe weather typically happens. This is useful because it will reflect the overall climate that these weather stations are located in. As these NOAA stations are located in the United States and Canada, it makes sense that there are more severe weather events in the summer months of June, July, and August as that is typically when these events occur.\n",

    "tfcArr = [float(i) for i in dataFrame['tornado_funnel_cloud']]\n",
    "tornadosSum = np.sum(tfcArr)\n",
    "\n",
    "hailArr = [float(i) for i in dataFrame['hail']]\n",
    "hailSum = np.sum(hailArr)\n",
    "\n",
    "thunArr = [float(i) for i in dataFrame['thunder']]\n",
    "thunderSum = np.sum(thunArr)\n",
    "\n",
    "totalCount = tornadosSum + hailSum + thunderSum\n",
    "\n",
    "x_list = [tornadosSum, hailSum, thunderSum]\n",
    "label_list = [\"Tornado\", \"Hail\", \"Thunder\"]\n",
    "\n",
    "pyplot.axis(\"equal\")\n",
    "pyplot.pie(\n",
    "        x_list,\n",
    "        labels=label_list,\n",
    "        autopct=\"%1.1f%%\"\n",
    "        )\n",
    "pyplot.title(\"Severe Weather Events\")\n",
    "pyplot.show()"
   ]

   "source": [
    "Now that we analyzed the severe weather events by time, lets break hail, tornados, and thunder down and see which is the most common since 1929. From the looks of this pie graph, it is clear that thunder related events are the most prevalent at 84% while hail and tornados account for the minority of severe weather events.\n",
    "\n",
    "As tornados are a rare severe weather event and mostly occur in midwestern/flat land areas, the weather station would have to be in such an area to report a tornado; therefore it makes sense that they account for only 0.4% of the total number of severe weather events overall. \n",
    "\n",
    "While this data set doesn't provide us a location in the country where each weather station is located, it more than likely that midwestern weather stations would report more severe weather events (such as hail and tornados specifically) than stations in other areas."

    "This code and line graph's goal is to lead us into the statistical analysis of this project. We want to see whether or not there is a correlation between temperature, wind, preciptation, and severe weather. By putting all the averages over time in one graph, it makes it much easier to see whether or not there is a correlation.\n",
    "\n",
    "From a quick (non-numerical) analysis, it appears that temperature, wind, and severe weather events are all somewhat correlated. It is important to note though, the severe weather line here is the SUM of the weather events for a particular year rather than the average. We would rather see the sum than the averages since the averages would be too low to see in a graph otherwise."

   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "The correlation coefficient matrix is symmetric, where the rows and columns both represent the weather events graphed above. Each cell represents the correlation between the row and column name (the diagonals are all ones because the cell and column name match, and there is a 100% correlation between any dataset and itself). Examining this matrix, it appears that the only two factors that are stronly correlated are temperature and severe weather events, at 72% correlation. The other characteristics seem to be only slightly correlated with each other, with the second largest correlation at 31% for temperature and precipitation, and all the rest much lower."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": true
   },
   "source": [
    "Now that we know that some weather events are correlated, let's see if we can find the weather characteristic which is most variable over time. To accomplish this, we will run principal component analysis. We first create a new dataframe consisting of the means of each numeric component of the data frame over an entire year; this way, variability across seasons does not affect the variability over the entire period of time we are analyzing. Next, we center the data so that we can calculate the covariance matrix. Finding the eigenvector associated with the largest eigenvalue of the covariance matrix yields the dimension of largest variability. In order to find which of our weather characteristics are closest to this dimension, we find the angles between this eigenvector and each of the characteristics. The characteristic with the smallest angle is closest to this dimension of variability, and therefore varies over time more than the rest."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 13,
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": false
   },
   "outputs": [
    {
     "name": "stdout",
     "output_type": "stream",
     "text": [
      "The weather attribute closest to the direction of most variability is Sea Level Pressure\n"
     ]
    }
   ],
   "source": [
    "dataFrame['Wind Speed'] = dataFrame['Wind Speed'].astype(np.float64).fillna(0)\n",
    "dataFrame['Max Wind Speed'] = dataFrame['Max Wind Speed'].astype(np.float64).fillna(0)\n",
    "\n",
    "numeric_components = ['Temperature', 'Dew Point', 'Sea Level Pressure', 'Station Pressure','Wind Speed' , \\\n",
    "                      'Daily Preciptation', 'Snow Accumulation']\n",
    "dataFrame['Year Num'] = dataFrame['Year'].astype(np.int64).fillna(0)\n",
    "\n",
    "centered = pd.DataFrame(columns = ('Temperature', 'Dew Point', 'Sea Level Pressure', 'Station Pressure','Wind Speed' , \\\n",
    "                                   'Daily Preciptation', 'Snow Accumulation'))\n",
    "for j in range(1929,2017):\n",
    "    row = np.zeros(len(numeric_components))\n",
    "    for i,e in enumerate(numeric_components):\n",
    "         row[i] = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year Num'] == j, e])\n",
    "    centered.loc[j-1929] = row\n",
    "\n",
    "centered = dataFrame[numeric_components]\n",
    "for i in numeric_components:\n",
    "        centered[i] = centered[i] - centered[i].mean()\n",
    "        \n",
    "evals, evecs = np.linalg.eig(centered.cov())\n",
    "indices = evals.argsort()[::-1]\n",
    "evals = np.array(evals)[indices]\n",
    "evecs = np.array(evecs)[:,indices]\n",
    "\n",
    "angles = np.zeros(len(numeric_components))\n",
    "for i in range(len(numeric_components)):\n",
    "    temp = np.zeros(len(numeric_components))\n",
    "    temp[i] = 1\n",
    "    angles[i] = np.arccos((np.dot(evecs[0], temp))/(np.linalg.norm(evecs[0])*np.linalg.norm(temp)))\n",
    "print(\"The weather attribute closest to the direction of most variability is\", numeric_components[angles.argsort()[0]])"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": true
   },
   "source": [
    "We find that the dimension that varies most is actually Sea Level Pressure. We haven't seen a visualization of how this varies over time, so let's take a look at a graph."
   ]

    "one = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1929', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "two = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1939', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "three = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1949', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "four = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1959', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "five = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1969', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "six = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1979', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "seven = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1989', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "eight = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '1999', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "nine = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2009', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "ten = ma.mean(dataFrame.loc[dataFrame['Year'] == '2017', 'Sea Level Pressure'])\n",
    "\n",
    "overallMeanArray = [one, two, three, four,\n",
    "                   five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten]\n",
    "\n",
    "x = timeArrayInt\n",
    "xi = np.asarray(timeArrayInt)\n",
    "slope, intercept, r_value, p_value, std_err = mstats.linregress(x,overallMeanArray)\n",
    "line1 = intercept + slope*xi\n",
    "\n",
    "overallPressureMeanArray = overallMeanArray\n",
    "\n",
    "plt.figure(figsize=(11,5))\n",
    "plt.plot(timeArray, overallMeanArray, '-b', linewidth=1.5, label='') #blue line\n",
    "plt.plot(xi,line1,'g-')\n",
    "plt.ylabel('Sea Level Pressure in Millibars')\n",
    "plt.xlabel('Year')\n",
    "plt.title('Sea Level Pressure over Time')\n",
    "plt.show()"

    "As shown in the graph above, we can now see that sea level pressure changes quite a lot over the years. As air pressure is an important factor in the formation of storms, this also makes sense with respect to the increasing number of severe weather events. Let's see if pressure is correlated with severe weather events."

    "Looking at this graph, as we predicted, it seems that as pressure decreases over time, severe weather events per year increase. To confirm this prediction, we calculate the correlation coefficient of these two characteristics, which is about -.66. This means that there is 66% inverse correlation between pressure and severe weather events, which is a moderately strong inverse correlation."
